Bha gu leòr againn na dhà fhéin a thoir’ innte; bha sinn sianar a’ tarraing nan dà lìon fhéin; sianar. Chaill sinn an còrr.

Bha mi ri giomaich a mach air an taobh an iar, agus rachadh sibh pìos math a mach o’n chladach a’ sin, mun rachadh sibh gu móran doimhneachd, mun rachadh sibh gu fichead aitheamh a dhoimhneachd. Bhiodh sinn a’ dol cho fad’ a mach air an taobh an iar, agus gu robh sinn a’ togail an taobh an ear, bun Loch Aoineort. Cha n-fhaicinn am fearann ìseal idir. Well, cha robh a’ sin a nist a dhoimhneachd a’ sin ach mu chóig aitheamh fhichead. ’S ann a mach a’ siod a gheobhte na giomaich toiseach an t-samhraidh; cha n-fhaighte sian a staigh aig a’ chladach tràth air a’ bhliadhna, tràth as t-samhradh. Na gheobhadh tu, ’s ann ’sa chuan. Nuair a bha iad a’ tighinn a staigh chon a’ chladaich, a staigh chon an tanalach, cha ruigte leas a dhol a mach cho fad’ siod, cha ruigeadh.

Bha sinn a toir’ a staigh na sgothadh mu’n cuairt Caolas Éirisgeidh, no a toir’ ro’n fhadhail air Beinne bhFhadhla. Nam biodh latha math ann agus a’ ghaoth bhith freagarrach, cha bhithte fad’ ’sa bith tighinn mu’n cuairt ann. [’S e] fear air fichead—fear no dhà is trì fichead [a chléibh] a bh’againne. ’S e Dòmhnall Fearghustan a bha ceannach nan giomach, ’s bhiomaid faighinn rudan as a’ bhùth aige. Cha robh sian a dh’iarradh iasgair nach robh aig Dòmhnall Fearghustan. Bha lìn, streangannan, taighn air son cléibh ghiomach, rud ’sa bith a dh’iarradh tu, gheobhadh tu ’n siod e. Bha e fasdadh nam bàtaichean a rithist, nan sgothan, fad an t-samhraidh, agus a’ chiad mhìos an fhoghmhair; gheobhadh sibh seachd tasdain an dusan orra; well, bhiodh iad beagan na b’fheàrr na sin fad a’ gheamhraidh, nuair nach biodh iad ann, nuair a bha iad a’ fàs gann; bha e an uair sin a’ toir’ ochd tasdain orra; uaireannan naoi tasdain. Ach dh’fheumadh iad ceithir òrlaich a bhith as a’ chom aige, mun seasadh e giomach; nam biodh e na bu lugha na sin, cha robh e seasamh ach leith-ghiomaich dhut; ’s ged a bhiodh traigh ann, ma bha té dha na h-ìnnean dh’a dhìth, cha seasadh e ach leith-ghiomaich. (4) Bha esan a’ deanamh fortan dha fhéin.

13 – 704538 Lochlann. Bd 5

[TD 194]

’S i creutair bu mhutha chuir a dh’eagal riamh orm, muc-mhara. Bha sinn oidhche a’ cur lìon, ann as t-fhoghmhair a bh’ann, cuideachd, mach ann am bun Loch Aoineort, agus bha sgadan gu leòr ann, agus ma bha, bha gu leòr as a dheoghaidh; bha mucan as a dheoghaidh, agus bha dìreach am muir beò le bioraich. Well, thòisich iad cur. Well, thànaig i siod am bàrr a nuas ri taobh na sgothadh againn, agus am buta a bha mi tilgeil a mach, bhuail e as a’ chliathaich oirre; agus dh’fhan i dìreach am bàrr, bha fad a’ lìn am bàrr dhi; agus an dà shùil a bh’innte, cha mhór nach robh iad urad ris an t-seithir ud; agus cho dubh! Cha robh teagamh mhàthar agam dìreach nach digeadh i fo’n sgothaidh. O, cha do chuir i dragh ’sa bith oirnn, ach bha i treis am bàrr steadaidh ’s chaidh i ’n sin fodha cho socair dìreach, ’s cha n-fhaca mi sealladh tuilleadh dhi. Ach deara chuir i sgràth gu leòr oirnn! Bha fad lìon sgadain, ochd aitheamh dhiag, innte; bha sinn am bàrr dhi, dìreach o bhuta gu buta. Bhuail mise buta oirre dìreach as a’ chliathaich; cha do chuir i diù ann. ’S i ’n té bu mhutha a chunna mi riamh. Ach gu deara, bha sinne gu math toilichte gun do dh’fhalbh i, gun do chlìor i air falbh.


67. SEALGAIREACHD (182)

Dh’fhalbh mi fhìn ’s MacAsgaill oidhche a shealgaireachd, agus bha sneachd ann, ’sa gheamhradh; agus ghabh sinn a staigh gu Cill Donain a’ siod, agus shìos aig a’Ròdha a’ sin aig a’ chladach, bhiodh móran do lachain a’ tighinn am an amm sneachda is reothaidh, a dh’iarraidh an uisge, agus seo far an robh sinn gus a dhol. Nuair a ràna sinn Cill Donain, chan e gun dorchnachadh ceart; agus bha iothlann aig an tuathanach shìos aig taobh na h-aibhneadh ann, agus bha cruachan feòir innte, aon dusan cruach fheòir, agus chaidh sinn a staigh dha’n iothlainn an lùib nan cruach, gus an dorchnaicheadh i.

Dhia, chunna sinn an seo speil lachain a’ tighinn, agus, o! laigh iad shìos dìreach aig bun na Ròdha as an uisge. ’S e sinn a bha air ar dòigh, ach dé na truaighe, thug sinn a’ seo an aire do dhithis ag ialadh sìos as a’ chladach air an taobh thall dha’n uisge, agus na coin ag ialadh as an deoghaigh. A Dhia, ’s e na geamciobairs a bha an seo; ach gu dé mar a gheobh sinn air falbh? no teicheadh?

Bha cù mór coilearach dubh (1) agamsa, ’s ann a Maighstir Iain fhéin a fhuair mi e ’na chuilein, agus bha e uabhasach math air a’ ghunna. Bha sinn a’ fuireach as an iothlainn fiach am fàsadh i dorcha agus nach

[TD 195]

fhaiceadh iad sinn, nam faigheamaid teicheadh a mach. Ach fhuair àsan an seo cothrom air a’ lachainn agus ligeadh air falbh le ceithir urchraichean a bha ’n siod. Ach leum na coin againn a mach seachad air a’ ghàrradh sìos dha’n chladach, ’s cha do rinn iad ach leum a mach air an taobh eile agus tarraing air a’ mhachaire, sìos air Àird Mhaoile. Bha dùil againn gum biodh àsan as ar deoghaidh, ’s bha dùil againn a h-uile mionaid gun cluinneamaid urchair a’ dol ris na coin; ach cha chuala.

Ach rug a’ seo an cù dubh oirnn sìos am machaire agus lach’ aige ’nabhial.

“Hala, laochain” arsa Seonaidh “thug thusa do chuid fhéin as a’ bhlàr!”

Chum sinn an uair sìos chon a’—gu Rubha Àird Mhaoile; cha ligeadh an t-eagal dhuinn urchair a losgadh tuilleadh fiach am faigheamaid clìor is am machaire, mun traceadh àsan sinn. Chum sinn ’n seo a nuas gu Bòrnais, agus nuair a bha sinn a nuas, gu rathad na h-eaglais ann a’ sin, bha flagais aig Iain Fearghastan shìos aig ceann na Ròdha aige, agus dé an truaighe a chunna sinn ach an dithis a bha ’n seo a nuas taobh an locha dha’r n-ionnsaigh. Cha robh àite falaich againn ach dh’fhalbh sinn ’s chaidh sinn dha’n fhlagais. O, gur h-ann straight dìreach a dh’ionnsaigh na flagais a chum an dithis a bha ’n seo; agus bhruidhinn a’ seo Maighstir Iain, ’s e a bhruidhinn:

“Bheil duine a’ seo?” ars esan.

Seo nuair dh’fhairich sinne có a bh’ann. Có an truaighe a bh’ann ach e fhéin agus mac Ruairidh ’ic Dhòmhnaill Òig! agus iad fhéin an déidh dhol gu bun na Ròdha! agus sin nuair a dh’fhoighneachd e dhinn:

“Nach e sìbh a bh’aig an Ròdha shuas?”

“O, ’s sinn” arsa mise.

“Dia gléidh mi” ars esan “car son nach deach sibh sìos far an robh sinn?”

“Bha dùil againne” arsa mise “gur h-e na geamciobairean a bh’ann.”

“Da, bha dùil againn” arsa esan “gur h-e na geamciobairs* bh’unnaibh péin, ’s e na coin agaibh bu luaithe a bha a mach na na coin againn fhìn! A’ chiad beothach air an d’fhuair e greim, cha b’ann ugainn a thànaig e leis, ach tarraing air an taobh eile, ’s cha n-fhaca sinne an ath-shealladh dheth. Ach mhill sibh sinn, agus mhill sibh sibh péin.”

Cha d’fhuair àsan sian, cha robh ach trì lachain a bh’aca. Cha d’fhuair iad sian ach na fhuair iad an uair iad fhéin; agus bha sinne toir’ dhaibh an lacha a thug an cù dubh shuas; o, cha ghabhadh iad idir i. “Bheir leat dhachaigh i.” Agus sin mar a dh’éirich dhuinn an oidhche sin, cha d’fhuair sinn móran co dhiù!

[TD 196]

68. AN DOBHARCHU (183)

Dh’fhalbh mi oidhche, mi fhìn agus Alasdair mac Iain a bh’ann, Alasdair Dòmhnallach ann an Loch Aoineort, nàbaidh dhomh, dh’fhalbh sinn a shealgaireachd air feadh na h-oidhche, is gealach bhriagha ann, agus sneachda; agus chaidh sinn a mach Bràigh’ Ormaicleit ann a’ shin, far a bheil mòin’ Ormaicleit a muigh as a’ mhonadh. Bha ligeadh (1) ann a’ shin eadar an dà locha, agus bhiodh geòidh a’ laighe ann a’ shin, is lachain, nuair a bhiodh reothadh is sneachd ann.

Nuair a ràna sinn sin, cha robh giadh no lach no sian ann. Shuidh sinn shuas aig bonn cnoic a bh’ann, bha seòrsa do locha aig ceann na ligidh ann, agus bha e fosgailte. Agus sin agad far am biodh na lachain a’ tighinn a dh’iarraidh uisge nuair a bhiodh an reothadh ann. Dh’fhalbh mise sìos gu taobh an locha ann a’ shin fiach am faighinn clach a chuirinn fodham as an t-sneachda gun a bhith ’gam fhliuchadh. Bha tobhta bheag cloicheadh ann am bòrd an locha, (2) tha mi smaointinn gur h-e àite as an robh iad a’ togail uisge-bheatha uaireigin a bh’ann, ’s ann ris a bha e coltach. A h-uile clach a bh’ann a dh’fhiachainn, bha iad cho cruaidh, bha iad air reothadh, agus—Ach nochd an ceann a bha ’n siod air taobh a muigh na tobhtadh agus mise ’na broinn, agus cha robh e dìreach sia ceumannan bhuam.

Ghabh i a staigh gu pongail dìreach agus dh’fhaodainn mo làmh a chur oirre an taobh a mach dha’n tobhta, agus sheas i ann a shin a’ coimhead orm, agus—well, ’s e biast dubh a bha mise smaointinn a bh’innte, ach bha i mór. Well, thànaig i air tìr air fad ach an t-urball; bha an t-urball aice as an uisge. Bhac mise an comhair mo chùil, cha robh rathad agam air guth a ràdh, fiach an doireadh am fear a bha ’na shuidhe shuas an gunna dhomh. Thòisich mi air smìdeadh air le m’ làimh air mo chùlaibh, agus mo cheann crom, agus thuig esan gu robh mi faicinn rudeigin; cha n-fhac esan sian, bha an tobhta eadar e is i.

Dh’fhalbh e agus thog e ’n gunna agus liùg e a nuas agus shìn e dhomh e air mo chùlaibh. Fhuair mise an gunna, agus bha dà urchair ann, ’s e miosal-lódair a bh’ann, cuideachd. Well, an déidh dhomh breith air a’ ghunna, b’fheudar dhomh bacadh trì cheumannan an comhair mo chùil leis cho clos ’s a bha i orm, cha b’urra mi gunna thogail rithe. Thog mi ’n gunna, ’s dh’fhiachadh an raight oirre, ’s cha n-fhalbhadh strad. As a’ mhionaid, dh’fhiach mi ’m barailt’ eile; cha do dh’fhalbh strad. Dé shaoil mi a bha e ach air a fhliuchadh, agus dh’fhalbh mi agus thilg mi dheth

[TD 197]

na cip agus fhuair mi dà cheap ùr air. Thogadh a rithist e, shnapadh a rithist an dà thrip as deoghaidh a chéile; cha n-fhalbhadh strad. Bheireadh an t-òrd gliong air a cheap mar gum buaileadh e air gàd iarainn.

“O, taigh na truaighe” arsa mise “tha a h-uile sian a th’ann air a bhàthadh.” Rinn mi tionndadh air falbh. Bhac i gu socair dìreach air falbh mar a thànaig i a staigh.

“Có ris” arsa Alasdair “a bha thu snapadh a’ siod?”

“Nach robh ri beist duibh?” arsa mise.

“Cà robh i?”

“Nach robh i air mo bhialaibh air bonn na tobhtadh?”

“Ah, cha b’e biast dubh a bh’ann!”

“Well, ’s e biast dubh a bha mi smaointinn a bh’ann, co dhiù” arsa mise.

“Cha n-e; feumaidh gura bana-bhuistreach a bh’ann!”

“Da, nam bithinn-sa smaointinn air a’ sin, gu robh mi air an t-airgead a chur ann. Ach ga brì gu dé ’n rud a bh’ann, faodaidh sinn a dhol dhachaigh, feumaidh gu bheil a h-uile sian a th’ann air a fhliuchadh.”

Ach as an amm, thànaig lach, is coileach, agus laigh iad dìreach air ar bialaibh ann an gathan na gealaich.

“Bheil urchair agad ’sa ghunna?” arsa mise.

“Cha n-eil” arsa Alasdair. “Nach fhiach thu orra?”

“Dé as fheàirrde mise fiachainn orra” arsa mise, “’s bha e diùltadh ceithir uairean?”

“Cha n-eil fhios a’m; nach fhiach thu orra?”

Dh’fhalbh mi ’s thog mi ’n gunna air a chomhairle is dh’fhiachadh orra, ’s mharbh mi le chéil’ iad! Dh’fhalbh an urchair eile a’ cheart cho cleabhair. Seo nuair a thàna mi ugam fhìn; gu dé a Dhia mhóir an rud a bha mise snapadh ris nuair nach lasadh ceap no rud eile? Agus ga brì gu dé ’n rud a bh’ann, cha do ghabh mise eagal ’sa bith roimhe, ach chuir e iaonadh gu leòr orm nach fhalbhadh urchair ris, gu feumadh nach e rud ceart a bh’ann!

Duine a bha pòsd’ aig mo phiuthar, bha e ann an Tirìodh, ùine mhór ’na chìobair, agus bhiodh gunn’ aige, ’s bhiodh e falbh uaireannan leis. Dh’fhalbh e an latha bha ’n seo shìos rathad a’ chladaich, agus gu dé chunnaig e ach biast dubh a’ tighinn air tìr. Fhuair e eadar i ’s a’ mhuir, agus chaidh i a staigh fo chloich mhóir ann am bràighe a’ chladaich. ’S ann a bha deagh-chothrom aige oirre, bha e suas a dh’ionnsaigh na cloicheadh, agus bha i coimhead air agus i a staigh fo’n chreig. Loisg i oirre, agus bha a’ bhiast dubh cho beò ’s a bha i reimhe. Loisg e ’n sin an

[TD 198]

ath-té oirre. Bha a’ bhiast dubh beò ’s i coimhead air. Sheas e eadar i ’s a’ mhuir, ’s cha n-fhaigheadh i a mach.

Loisg e seachd urchaichean oirre; ’s ann leis a’ bhat’ a mharbh e mu dheireadh i, agus bha e ’g radha, ma bha dobharchu riamh ann, gum b’e siod e. Nuair a bha e ’ga feannadh, gun togadh e ’n luaidhe as a’ ghaoiseid aice le a mheòirean, cha deach sìlein riamh roimpe dhi, bha i stuigeadh as a’ chalg! (3)


69. ALASDAIR MÓR ’S AN STOIRM (185)

Chaidh mi fhìn agus Ailein mac ’Ille Mhaoil oidhche eile a choimhead air Alasdair Mór. Bha àirigh a muigh, àite dha’n can iad Na h-Ann a’ siod, bha port ann, far am biodh na h-iasgairean a’ tighinn leis na bàtaichean a staigh ann. Thànaig sinn a staigh ann a’ shin, ’s bha sinn dol a dh’fhuireach as an àirigh; cha robh againn ach bàta beag. Nuair a ràna sinne, cha robh ceann air an àirigh idir. Cha robh na b’fheàrr ’san oidhche bh’ann ach tarraing air taigh Alasdair Mhóir. Dh’fhàgadh an sgoth ann a’ shin. Dh’fhàg sinn na lìn air an cur.

Far an robh an taigh aige, bha e aig bonn sgaladh, agus ged a bhiodh gale ann, cha n-fhaireachadh tu, nam biodh tu staigh, gu robh gaoth idir ann. Gu dé rinn i ach sioftadh, agus thànaig i ’na stoirm o’n deas ’s o’n eardheas, air aghaidh a’ chladaich. Bha dùil againne gu robh an oidhche b’àille ann, ’s cha robh sinn ach ag éisdeachd naidheachdan Alasdair Mhóir, ’s na sgialachdan. Bha an rud a’ còrdadh ruinn uabhasach math.

Nuair a shoilleirich i ’n sin, dh’fhairich sinn a’ ghaoth a’ tighinn cho làidir nuair a thàna sinn a mach a taigh Alasdair Mhóir. Cha ghléidhinn an ceap mu m’ cheann le stoirm. Nuair a thàna sinn a nall far na dh’fhàg sinn an sgoth, bha ’n sgoth air a dhol fodha, bha i air a lìonadh. Cha robh fios againne fo’n t-saoghal gu dé ’n rud a dhianamaid. Bha na ràimh ’s an seòl ’s an crann shuas air tìr. Bha dùil againn gu robh i air a milleadh ’s gu robh i air a tolladh, ’s gu robh sinn dìreach ullamh. Bha na lìn, cha n-fhaiceamaid idir iad, cha robh sgialachd orra, bha iad air tarraing a mach chon na doimhneachd, ’s i ’na gale o’n iarthuath.

Ach co dhiù, fhuair sinn an sgoth a thoir’ gu tìr, agus fhuair sinn a toir’ am bàrr. Cha do rinneadh sian oirre, bha i sàbhailte gu leòr. Fhuair sinn a h-uile sian innte. Well, bha na h-acraichean—’s ann air acraichean a bha na lìn againn—bha na h-acraichean ann an greim ann am bodha a bha a mach pìos o bhun an locha, tuath aig bun an locha. Fhuair sinn na lìn,

[TD 199]

sàbhailte, ach cha robh sian unnta. Agus bha sinn searbh gu leòr a sgialachdan Alasdair Mhóir! Cha robh dad an asgaidh againn dhiù nuair a thill sinn, agus an sgoth a thoir’ am bàrr, agus na lìn fhaighinn!


70. ALASDAIR MÓR ’S NA H-OTHAISGEAN (186)

Bha mi fhìn agus Alasdair Dòmhnallach ann an Loch Aoineort agus Ailein Mac ’ille Mhaoil, bha sinn a’ cur lìon air sgadain as an amm, as a’ gheamhradh. Bha an oidhche car briagha, ’s nuair a chuir sinn na lìn a mach ann am bun Loch Aoineort, cha robh sian na b’fheàrr na thogadh a staigh an sgoth do dh’àite sàbhailte, ’s chuireadh acair’ oirre, ’s ann a rachamaid a thaigh Alasdair Mhóir a chùl na beinneadh, a null tarsainn na beinneadh.

’S e seo a rinn sinn, agus nuair a ràna sinn bha Alasdair agus a bhean a staigh, agus duine no dithis dha’n chloinn. Ach rinn Alasdair toileachadh mór ruinn, agus thòisich na naidheachdan aig Alasdair, agus na sgialachdan. Agus cha deach e leabaidh fad na h-oidhcheadh, ach ag innse naidheachdan air an duin’ ud ’s air an duin’ ud eile, mar a bha ’g éirigh dha fhéin, ’s mar a bhiodh am manaidsear a’ fiachainn ris. Gnothaichean dha’n t-seòrsa sin. Bha am manaidsear, fear Dòmhnallach a bh’ann an Ormaicleit an uair sin, gu robh e fuathasach cruaidh, agus gun do chuir Raghnall Dòmhnallach, ’s ann aige a bha Ormaicleit, chuir e dhachaigh leithchiad do dh’othaisgean, agus thug am manaidsear aire-san an toir’ leis a mach chùl na beinneadh, agus thuirt e ris nan doireadh e staigh uil’ iad, aig a’ Chaingis, gu faigheadh e pùnnd tombàca a bharrachd air a thuarasdal.

“Co dhiù” ars Alasdair “bha mi fhìn ’gam faicinn a h-uile latha, agus an trip a bha ’n seo, dé, dh’ionndrainn mi té dhiu; cha robh sgial agam oirre. O, shiubhail mi gach àite ’s cha d’fhuair, feumaidh gu h-ann a dhol leis a’ chladach a rinn i. Ah, cha robh cothrom air, ach gu robh teans nach fhaighinn-sa am pùnnd tombaca” ars Alasdair “nuair a chaill mi an othaisg. Ach fhuair mi ’n seo fios air son an toir’ a staigh gu ruig’ Ormaicleit. Dh’fhalbh mi agus an othaisg bu ghiobaiche agus bu choltaiche a chunna mi dha’n fheadhainn a bha ann an Ormaicleit, fhuair mi greim oirre agus chuir mi smod do chìl (1) oirre far an robh i air càch, agus thug mi leam i. Nuair a ràna mi, chuir mi a staigh do shead iad, agus chaidh mi a staigh dha’n taigh.

Nuair a thànaig am manaidsear a nuas:

[TD 200]

“Tha thu air tighinn, Alasdair” ars esan.

“Tha” arsa mi fhìn.

“An dug thu staigh na h-othaisgean?”

“Thug.”

“Bheil iad agad uile?”

“Tha.”

“Hugainn dha’m faicinn, ma thà.”

Dh’fhalbh sinn a mach as an uair. Bha e dol rompa ’s a’ coimhead orra.

“Da, cha chreid mi fhin, Alasdair” ars esan “gur h-ann dhiù tha i siod idir.”

“’S ann dhiù tha i sin gu deara” arsa mi fhìn, “nach eil a’ smod oirre fhéin mar a tha i air càch?”

“O, leanadh cìl ri clòimh ’sa bith, Alasdair.”

“O, cha n-eil sin ach air son am pùnnd tombàca chumail bhuam, ’s feumaidh tu am pùnnd a thoir’dhomhsa ge b’oil leis an amhaich agad!”

Well fhuair mi am pùnnd, arsa Alasdair, ach bha teagamh aige innte. Bha teagamh aig’ innte. ’S e fìor-rasgal a bh’ann. Bha mi trip eil’ aig amm breith nan uan, dh’éirich mi madainn a bh’ann a’ sheo, ’s bha an t-uisg’ ann, fad na h-oidhche. Nuair a sheall mi a mach, chunna mi, gu dé chunna mi ach an ad aig a’ mhanaidsear ghlas ’gam fhàgail shuas ann an Gleann Thormasgol os cionn an taighe. Dé, thuirt mi gu robh an t-amm agam dìreach teicheadh. Cha do rinn mi ach mo sheacaid a chàradh orm ’s tarraing cho luath ’s a rinn mi riamh a nall ris a’ chladach. Nuair a bha mi dol seachad air abhainn Thearasdail, chuir mi dhìom an t-seacaid, agus bhog mi as an abhainn i. Chum mi ’n seo romham a null, agus thug esan an aire dhomh, agus ghabh e nuas far an robh mi.

“Tha sibh air éirigh, Alasdair” thuirt e.

“Ma thà, tha treis o’n uair sin a dh’éirich mise, agus cha chreid mi nach eil treis o’n a dh’éirich thu fhéin cuideachd ma bheil thu ’n seo an dràsd.”

“Tha treis o’n dh’éirich mise gu deara, cha n-eil sibhse ach air éirigh.”

“Da, tha sin glé chollach ri m’urball” (2) arsa mi, ’s mi breith air an t-seacaid ’s dh’fhàisg mi i.

“Aye” ars esan “tha sibh fliuch!”

“Tha mi fliuch, ge tà.”

“Cà robh sibh?”

“Chaidh mi a mach dha’n Ghleann Dorcha.”

“Da, ’s ann a mach mar sin a thàna mise, ’s cha d’fhairich cù no sian.”

“Cha robh feum agamsa air a bhith ’g oibreachadh coin ann.”

[TD 201]

Thànaig e chon an taighe còmhla rium” arsa Alasdair, “’s chuir mi dhìom an t-seacaid; ma bha e air breith orm a staigh an latha sin, cha robh agam ach an rathad mór!”

Bha mi fhìn cóig bliadhna fichead an uair ud.


71. MAR A GHLÉIDH MI MO CHUID CHAORACH (188)

Cha robh caoirich againn ann an Loch Aoineart idir an toiseach, nuair a bha an tuathanach ann, ach fhad ’s a bha Mac ’ill’ Sheathain ann, Teàrlach MacLean, bha cead againn air caoirich a chumail air a’ mhonadh aig an fhear sin: Agus bhithte ’g obair dha. Nuair a chuir am fear sin bhuaidh’ e, thànaig a’ sin an Dòmhnallach ann, ’s ann a Barraidh a bha esan, mun dànaig e gu ruige Gearrabhailteas; ’s ann aige bha Bhàtarsaidh. Well, cha robh caora ri bhith aig duine air an àite ach aige fhéin, aig an fhear sin. Cha robh gin dha na cìobairean nach robh caoirich aca, agus dh’fheumadh iad an cur bhuatha.

Well, na caoirich a bh’againne, ’s ann thall air a’ mhonadh aca bhiodh iad, agus cha robh teans idir agamsa air caora a bhith agam mura faigheadh duine dha na bh’air an àit’ aige fhéin an cumail. Ach co dhiù bha cìobair aige bha a mach taobh Loch Aoineart, ’s e Crìslein (1) a bh’air, agus nuair a bhiodh trusadh gus a bhith ann, bheireadh e brath dhomhsa gus na caoirich a thoir’ a null chon an taighe, gun an ligeil a staigh dha’n fhang. Agus bha mise toir’ a null nan caorach, agus cha robh sian a dh’fhios aige gu robh gin air a’ mhonadh aige. Agus bha manaidsear aige, fear Sgiathanach, Niall Peutan a chanadh iad ris, do leath-amadan. Bha e gu math na bu mhiosa na bha a mhaighstir, co dhiù, nam faigheadh e a dh’ionnsaigh duine.

Ach co dhiù an trip a bh’ann a’ sheo, bha mise a’ rùsgadh ann an Gearrabhailteas a staigh, chuireadh fios ugam gus a dhol a rùsgadh. Agus ’s e crogaichean a’ mhachaire a bh’againn, ’gan rùsgadh, agus bha iad sin fuathasach doirbh, feadhainn aca làn gainmheachadh. Bha am Peutanach a’ smaointinn nach robh gin idir ann a bha cho math ris fhéin gu rùsgadh. An latha bh’ann a’ sheo, thànaig e a mach agus ann an deagh-thrim, agus shuidh e as a’ chiad chaora fhuair e, ’s bha mise ’nam shuidhe air fuirm eile m’a choinneamh thall.

“Hùidseadh ort, (2) a Mhic ’ill’ ’Ialain” ars esan.

Cha do shaoil mi fhìn sian dheth, ach thuirt mi nach robh e ach ri fun an toiseach. Nuair a bha i ullamh aige, dh’éibh e “Siod a h-aon” ars esan.

[TD 202]

“Well, ma thà,” arsa mise “cha n-fhada gus am bi h-aon an seo cuideachd.”

Bhuaileadh a mach i, agus fhuair sinn a dhà eile. Ach mu robh esan leathach dha’n dàrna té, bha i as a’ chlobhs’ agamsa. “Siod a dhà” arsa mise. “Tha a dhà gus a bhith an seo cuideachd. Tha an t-amm aige cabhag a dheanamh” arsa mise. Mun do chuir esan a mach an dàrna té, bha an treas té agamsa a mach còmhla rithe!

“O” arsa mise “cha dànaig e as an Eilein, fear a ruigeas a leas fiachainn. Faodaidh tu stad a nist.”

O, an truaighe diog a thuirt e, ’s dh’fhàs e cho dearg as an aghaidh! Dh’fhalbh e an uair sin ’s leum e a nall far an fhuirm. “Am faic mi an siosar agad?” ars esan. “Siod e” arsa mise. Rug e air ’s dh’fhiach e e. “Cha n-eil a leithid as a’ lod” ars esan.

“Nach math a’ làmh ’na thachair e?” arsa mise.

“Cha b’urrainn dhi bhith na b’fheàrr” ars esan.

Bha sinn a’ seothach gus a bhith ullamh dha na crogaichean tràth. Thànaig esan a mach agus thuirt e ri Crìslein:

“Hala thusa, a Chrìslein, agus bheir a nuas na bheil far an rathaid mhóir, an taobh-sa ceann na beinne, agus mu’n mhuileann, agus bidh na’s lugh’ againn ri dheanamh am màireach.” Thionndaidh e riumsa:

“A bheil an cù agad?” ars esan.

“Cha n-eil” arsa mise.

“Car son nach dug thu leat an cù” ars esan.

“Cha robh feum ’sa bith agamsa air cù an seo.”

“Da, th’agad ri dol a thrusadh as a’ mhadainn” ars esan.

“O, ma thà” arsa mise “nach eil an cù a muigh romham? Cà’il sibh gus a bhith trusadh?”

“Tha iad an Loch Aoineart” ars esan.

A Dhia, nuair a chuala mise seo, agus na caoirich agam muigh ’sa mhonadh! Bhiodh a h-uile mèath (3) dhiu as an fhang am màireach! Cha do lig mi sian orm, ach nuair a bha Crìslein a’ falbh, thuirt mi ris:

“O, na doir thu staigh ach cho beag ’s as urrainn dhut, fiach am faigh mise air falbh tràth. Cuir an cù riu a mach ris a’ mhonadh.”

“Da, masa h-urra mi” ars esan.

Dh’fhalbh Crìslein agus thànaig e agus aon dà chiad aige! agus dhùraiginn a bhruich nuair a chunna mi na thug e staigh dhiu, agus a’ ghrian a’ cromadh gu math. Well, thuirt mi rium fhìn ma bha teans agam air gin fhaighinn, gur h-ann an nochd, cha ruiginn a leas a dhol a dh’fhiachainn.

[TD 203]

am faighinn ’sa mhadainn. O, thòisich mi air rùsgadh cho trang ’s a b’urrainn mi, siod an oidhche bha e ag radha gun do ghabh mi an caothach!

“O, tha e air a’ chaothach a ghabhail” ars esan.

Bha a’ ghrian dìreach a’ dol fodha nuair a bha sin ullamh. Dh’fhoighneachd mi dhiu cuin a bhiodh iad a’ falbh a thrusadh.

“O, tha mi cinnteach” ars esan “nach bi do dhàil aca ach gus an gabh iad am biadh.”

“O well,” arsa mise “ged a dh’fhalbhadh tu as a’ bhad as a bheil thu, ma thà, bidh mise romhad.”

“O well” ars esan “tha staoil ann am Mac ’ill’ ’Ialain!”

Cha b’ann air an taigh a thug mise m’aghaidh ach air a’ bheinn. Well, fhuair mi a h-uile gin dhiu ach aointé, gun chù gun rud eile ach na pluic! agus dhrive mi chon an taigh’ iad, Agus nuair a ràna mi, bha mo mhàthair, sìth Dhia dha h-anam, ’s bha i ’gam fheitheamh, agus bha e an uair sin uair ’sa mhadainn. Las mi ’n lainnteir agus chrochadh as a’ bhàthaich e, agus thàirneadh a staigh dha’n bhàthaich an dab aca. (4) Dhùineadh an dorus. B’fheudar dhomh a h-uile snaithle a bh’orm a chur dhìom, agus aodach tioram a chur orm. Dh’fhàisgeadh tu a h-uile stitch aodaich a bh’orm le fallus. Nuair a ghabh mi mo bhiadh an uair sin, cha robh mi ach tarraing air a’ bheinn air ais. Well, cha robh mi sian ach dìreach air ceann a muigh dha’n mhonadh a ruighinn, dìreach mi air suidhe aige a’ lasadh na pìoba, nuair a chuala mi an sgread aigesan gu h-àrd air taobh na beinne os mo chionn. Thug an cù air tòiseachadh air comhartaich. Chuala esan e.

“Ha, tha thu ann!” ars esan.

“Tha” arsa mise “cha do rinn thusa an gnothach fhathast orm.”

Nuair a fhuair mi seachad air an trip a bha ’n sin, Dhia, thuirt mi rium fhìn gun digeadh là air choireigin a dheanadh e an gnothach orm, ’s gum b’fheàrr dhomh fhìn a dhol agus a reportadh, na bhith ligeil le duin’ eile dol dh’a reportadh, gu robh iad ann. Thàna mi a staigh gu ruige Gearrabhailteas. Thachair an nighean aige rium a muigh, ’s dh’fhoighneachd mi dhi an robh h-athair a staigh, ’s thuirt i gu robh. agus “thig a staigh” ars ise. Chaidh mi a staigh, ’s bha esan shìos as a’ rùm, ’s chaidh mi sìos. Thànaig e a nuas, O, duine còir! Shuidh e.

“Dé tha sibh a’ deanamh an Loch Aoineart, Aonghuis?” ars esan.

“O, cha n-eil móran” arsa mise.

“An ann riumsa a bha turus agaibh?” ars esan.

[TD 204]

“’S ann” arsa mise.

“Dé ’n turus a th’ann?” ars esan.

“Beagan chaorach a th’agam” arsa mise “air a’ mhonadh agaibh, agus mura h-urrainn duibhs’ àit’ a thoir’ dhaibh ann, ’s ann air a’ mhonadh agaibh a bha iad air an togail riamh, feumaidh mi an cur uam, cha n-eil àit’ agam fhìn dhaibh.”

“Bheil cus ann?” ars esan.

“Tha dha dhiag ann aig a bheil uain” arsa mise, “is beagan do sheasgaich eile còmhla riu.”

“O, well, Aonghuis, air son an urad sin, cha bhi am monadh ach mar a bha e reimhe. Fàg thus’ ann iad o’n a tha iad ann. Tha sinne cur dragh ort fhéin.”

“O, ma thà” arsa mise “sian ’sa bith bhios sibhse ’g iarraidh air an son, bidh mise glé leagte ri thoir’ dhuibh.”

“O, gu dearbh” ars esan “tha thu deanamh feum mhór dhuinn. Fàg thus’ ann iad, Aonghuis.”

Fhuair mise cead am fàgail ann, ’s cha n-fhaigheadh duine dha na bh’air a’ bhaile aointé a chumail!


72. CÙIRT NAN CON (191)

Seo a’ bhliadhna chaidh na cruitean a ghearradh dhuinn ann an Loch Aoineort, dha’n t-seann-fheadhainn a bh’ann. ’S ann aig an tacksman a bha an t-àite roimhe sin, ’s cha robh sinn ach a’ pàigheadh dha’n tacksman. Thànaig teanant ùr gu ruige Ormaicleit, fear a mhuinntir Mhuile, agus sgrìobh sinn an uair sinn a dh’ionnsaigh Lady Cathcart fiach am faigheamaid an t-àite an robh sinn a ghearradh a mach dhuinn fhìn ’na chruitean, nam b’e toil an tuathanaich e.

Well, chuir an Leddi fios chon a’ bhàillidh e thighinn a shealltainn gu dé an t-àite a bh’aig na daoine a bh’ann an Loch Aoineort, ’s gu robh ise faicinn freagarrach gu leòr a ghearradh dhaibh ma bha an tuathanach deònach dealachadh ris. Well, thànaig am bàillidh a mach a choimhead an àite, ’s bha am fear sin ’ga fhaicinn freagarrach gu leòr cuideachd. Well, bha an tuathanach deònach gu leòr an ligeil dh’an ionnsaigh; ge bè rent a rachadh air, bha e air a thoir’ dheth-san. Chaidh an uair sin na cruitean a ghearradh dhaibh. Bha sinn ann naoinear ann an Loch Aoineort an uair sin.

Bha iad a’ fhaighinn lìbhrigeadh unnt’ (1) o’n bhàillidh, dh’fheumadh iad

[TD 205]

saighnigeadh aig a’ bhàillidh, nach biodh cù idir aca. Shaighnig feadhainn aca ceart gu leòr, a h-uile gin aca mu dheireadh, agus bha gu leòr dhiu fhéin aig nach robh sian a dh’fhios aca dé rud a bha iad a’ saighnigeadh. Agus nuair a chruinnich iad, chuir iad an comhairle ri chéile ’s a chunnaig iad ’n rud a rinn iad, gu feumadh iad—nach fheumadh iad cù a bhith idir aca. Well, cha robh e furasda dhaibh an gnothach a dheanamh gun chù, agus gu h-àraid cha robh e furasda dhomhsa e dheanamh, a thaobh bha caoirich agamsa, ’s cha robh rathad agam air an cruinneachadh no falbh leo ’gan dipeadh no a dh’àit’ eile mura biodh cù agam.

’S ann a dh’fheumamaid a’ seo sgrìobhadh chon a’ bhàillidh air ais chor ’s gu feumamaid ar saighnigeadh fhaighinn air ais, a thaobh nach robh fhios againn gu dé rud a bha sinn a’ saighnigeadh, as an amm. Well, chuir e litir ugainn gu robh esan a’ faicinn—gu dé an aon fheum a bhiodh againn air cù? ach gun cuireadh esan air bhialaibh Lady Cathcart e, agus air bhialaibh a’ Bhòrd of Agriculture. Bha seo ceart gu leòr, chaidh seo seachad. Ach glé ghoirid as a dheoghaidh sin, fhuair sinn sumanadh air son a dhol gu ruige Loch nam Madadh air son nan con, a h-uile duine bha ’san dùthaich. Cha robh fios fo’n t-saoghal gu dé an aon rud a dhianamaid, ach gu feumte nochdadh.

Well, ghabh sinn an stìomair sìos, agus an fheadhainn a bha tuath sìos ’s e na fadhlaichean a ghabh iad; agus bha droch-shìde ann as an amm cuideachd. Bha gu leòr dhiu a bha gu math fliuch mun d’fhuair iad chon nam fadhlaichean. Well, bha fear-lagh’ ann an Loch nam Madadh, an Dòmhnallach, agus chaidh iad far an robh am fear sin; ghabh am fear sin an ceus aca, gu rachadh e staigh air an taobh.

Well, ’s e Wilson a bh’aig Lady Cathcart, agus aig bàillidh a’ Chinn-a-Tuath, ’s aig bàillidh na h-Earradh; bha na trì oighreachdan a staigh còmhladh, agus ’s e Wilson a bh’aca sin ag obair. Bha Wilson gu math gleusda. Chaidh iad a Barraidh a null ann, triùir as a h-uile baile, agus sin a bha an Dòmhnallach ag iarraidh as a’ Cheann-a-Deas, agus chaidh triùir againn a Loch Aoineort ann, agus bha mise air fear dhiu. (2) Agus nuair a ràna sinn, thug an Dòmhnallach—Choinnich e sinn, ’s thug e a staigh dha’n oifis aige fhéin sinn. Thuirt e ruinn sinn a sheasamh cruaidh, nach cailleamaid na coin idir.

Dh’inns sinn dha gun sgrìobh sinn chon a’ bhàillidh a dh’iarraidh ar saighnigeadh air ais, gun do chuir e ugainn litir.

“Bheil còpaidh agaibh” ars esan “dha’n litir a chuir sibh dha’ ionnsaigh?”

[TD 206]

“Tha.”

“Tha mi glé thoilichte, ma thà” ars esan. “Fiach a’ seo e.”

Agus thug sinn dha a’ litir a thànaig o’n bhàillidh.

“Ah, well” ars esan “ligidh sinn leis a nist, fiach gu dé rud a nì e, ma thà.”

“Da” arsa mise “tha muinntir Bharraidh an déidh shaighnigeadh nach bi cù idir aca. Shaighnig iad do Wilson ’s tha iad air faighinn air falbh leis a’ stìomair a null. Dh’fheumamaid fuireach dà latha thall.”

Thionndaidh esan dhomh. “Mac an diabhoil” ars esan. “Ach ma ghabhas sibhse mo chomhairle, gabhaidh sibh leis an rud a nì an Siorram dhuibh, am màireach.”

“Deara fhéin, gabhaidh sinne, ’s collach gum bi sinn ceart gu leòr.”

Seo là’r-na-mhàrach, rinn sinn sin, ’s chaidh sinn a staigh dha’n chùirt. Thànaig Wilson dubh, thànaig e a staigh e fhéin, ’s an Dòmhnallach; thànaig a’ sin an Siorram a nuas, chon a’ bheins. Thòisich Wilson air plìodadh nach robh sian a dh’fheum aig na cruiteirean air cù; dh’fhaodadh iad—gu faigheadh iad cead ceithir choin, no trì choin, a chumail as a h-uile baile, ’s gun deanadh sin an gnothach. Thuirt an Dòmhnallach:

“Gu dé mar” ars esan “a nì sinn an gnothach? ’N e bhòtadh fiach có aige bhios na coin a nì iad? Masa h-e, bhòtaidh a h-uile fear air son cù a bhith aige fhéin. No an e cruinn a chur a mach, fiach có air a thig iad? Faodaidh an crann tighinn air an droch-dhuine ’s air an droch-cù. Dh’fhiach mise sin mar thà, agus dh’fhairlich orm gu faighinn dòigh a chur fhathast air.”

Cha robh an Siorram ach ag éisdeachd ris. Thug a’ seo an Siorram uige leabhar, agus bha e coimhead air. Leubh e ’n seo a mach an t-Achd a chaidh a phasadh, ’s an date air an deach e phasadh: gu faigheadh a h-uile cruiteir cù a chumail le exemption, a h-uile tuathanach, bha e ’g ràdh, le exemption.

Dh’éirich Wilson ’na aghaidh an uair sin, agus thuirt e:

“’N e tuathanach” ars esan “a chanas sibh ri cruiteir?”

“’S e” ars esan “ma tha barrachd is aona chaora is mart aige, ’s e oibreachadh a’ ghrùnn air a bheil iad, sin an tuathanach” ars esan. “Tha e collach gu faigh a h-uile duine” ars esan “aig a bheil spréidh, gu faigh iad cù a chumail, mar sin.” Sheall esan air an leabhar, agus thuirt e: “Well, tha e ’san Achd air innse air son duine sa bith, aig a bheil stoc, gu faigh e cù a chumail le exemption” ars esan, ’se tionndadh ris.

[TD 207]

“Well” arsa Wilson “tha gu leòr a’ cumail coin nach eil sgrìobht’ ann am fearann idir.” (3)

“O, well, ma tha stoc aca” arsa an Siorram “feumaidh gu bheil iad ’gan cumail an àiteigin, ma tha stoc aca.”

Thug am bàillidh an uair sin uige an leabhar, a h-uile duine aig an robh cruit as a’ Cheann-a-Deas. Thug cléireach an t-Siorraim uige a h-uile duine bha faighinn licence, agus bha cléireach an t-Siorram a’ leughadh a mach a h-uile duine is iad aig an robh licence. Nuair a bha e tighinn a dh’ionnsaigh fear nach robh fearann idir aige, bha am bàillidh ag éibheach nach robh am fear sin sgrìobht’ ann am fearann idir. Cha robh e sian ach ’ga chur air a mhionnan as a’ bhad as a’ robh e, dìreach. Bha e an uair sin a’ faighinn a cheasnachadh o’n t-Siorram; bheil stoc aige? bheil caoirich aige? beothaichean aige?

Dh’fhoighneachd MacAilfreis (4) “bheil muc aige?” arsa MacAilfreis. Bha an gnothach a’ dol air aghaidh mar sin. Dhia, cha do sgur Wilson an deoghaidh sin; bha Wilson determined gu rachadh an gnothach leis.

Thionndaidh an Siorram ris an uair sin, agus thuirt e:

“Well” ars esan “nam biodh e as an Achd nach fhaodadh Mr Tòmas Wilson a bhith ’g obair ’na aghaidh, no nam biodh e ann” ars esan “gu faodadh e: ach théid againn air Mr Tòmas Wilson a chur ’na shuidhe leis an Achd.”

Leubh e mach an uair sin a rithist an t-Achd dhaibh, ach fiach thusa na shuidh esan an deoghaidh sin! O, dh’fhàs e gu math crosda, an Siorram, an uair sin, agus thog e a làmh, agus:

“Well, faodaidh tu” ars esan “a bhith preachadh fhad’ ’s a thogair thu, ach cha déid thu na’s fhaide na siod” ars esan, ’s e bualadh a làimh’ air an leabhar, Cha b’urrainn e iarraidh air fuireach sàmhach! Bha esan cho math ris fhéin.

Well, fhuair sinn an uair sin clìor a mach, ach gu robh rule ann gu feumadh tu coilear a chumail or ’o chù, agus t’ainm as a’ choilear, air chor agus nan deanadh e cron, ’s gu faight’ e, gum biodh fhios có bu leis e. Bha sin ceart gu leòr.

Nuair a chaidh an sin an Land Bill a phasadh, fhuair sinn an uair sin monadh air son chaorach, agus bha mise an uair sin ag obair air an fhearann, ’s ann bu mhutha bha mi deanamh a dh’obair, na air an iasgach, ach bhithinn ag iasgach a h-uile samhradh, agus ag obair air an fhearann an còrr dha’n bhliadhna. Agus nuair a bhàsaich a’ sin m’athair, cha robh

[TD 208]

còmhla rium an uair sin ach mo mhàthair. Bha mi cumail air aghaidh, bha sinn cumail chaorach, agus bha sinn a’ faighinn air aghaidh math gu leòr; ach bhàsaich an uair sin mo mhàthair. Chum mise air aghaidh an uair sin bliadhna no dhà an déidh bàs mo mhàthar; cha robh mi fhìn a’ gléidheadh mo shlàinte ro-mhath an uair sin, ’s b’fheudar dhomh an stoc a chur bhuam, ach na caoirich; agus sin nuair a retire mise agus a thàna mise gu ruige Fròbost. Agus sin agaibh roinn dha’n chaitheamh-bheatha agamsa! (5)

[TD 209]

<eng>
NOTES

For abbreviations, see Bibliography.

<gai>I. UIBHIST A DEAS<eng> (SOUTH UIST)

<gai>1. Laithean m’Òige<eng> (Boyhood on South Uist)

The first two paragraphs were recorded on January 12th, 1960, the beginning of a short account of his life that Angus MacLellan recorded as an introduction to Stories from South Uist The paragraph about his schooldays was recorded on 6/5/60. The story about the fishing boat that brought the rum from the Isle of Man was recorded on 21/4/60.

1. <gai>Biurla<eng> in my transcription corresponds to [bjø:Lə] recorded by Professor Carl Hj. Borgstrøm, see Dialects of the Outer Hebrides, p. 37. The phonetic transcription used in these Notes is broader than Borgstrøm’s.
2. <gai>Coimhearspa<eng> = ‘waiting for the other man to do it first’, see entry in Fr. Allan McDonald’s Gaelic Words from South Uist.


<gai>2. Am Bàta thànaig a Éirinn<eng> (The Boat from Ireland)

1. <gai>toir<eng> is usual in Angus MacLellan’s speech for toirt.
2. <gai>àsan = iadsan.<eng>
3. <gai>mionaid<eng> ‘a minute’ is the smallest division of time used, so might also be translated ‘moment’.
4. The words in square brackets here were added by Angus MacLellan’s sister Mrs Campbell (Mór Bean Nìll), who was 100 years old on August 4th, 1967.


<gai>3.Calbhaidh<eng> (Calvay)
Recorded on 25/4/61.

1. <gai>A bha gabhail roimhe<eng> = who was acting as his housekeeper.
2. <gai>Ciad-bhainne<eng> = beestings, the first milk of a cow after calving, not very appetizing.
3. The text printed from here to the end of this section is from a different recording (that made on 9/8/60).
4. <gai>aith-bhliadhna,<eng> ‘next year’. Both this form, stressed on the second syllable, and <gai>aithbhliadhn(a),<eng> stressed on the first occur. Distinguished by being written in this way.


<gai>4. An Caitheamh-bheatha a bh’againne<eng> (How we used to live)

1. ea is pronounced eo in a number of words in Uist Gaelic, e.g. <gai>feabhas, feadhainn, leabhar, leaghadh, meabhail, treabhadh.<eng>

14 – 704538 Lochlann. Bd 5

[TD 210]

2. <gai>Ola mhór<eng> is the old South Uist word for paraffin—now usually called <gai>paramainn<eng> or <gai>parafainn.


5. Am Fiabhras Mór<eng> (The Fever)
Recorded on 6/7/60.


<gai>6. Mar a fhuair mi mo sheachd othaisgean<eng> (How I found my Seven Hoggs)

1. <gai>tearbadh<eng> = weaning.
2. <gai>geamciobair<eng> = gamekeeper, stress on first syllable, short i: [gʹmkʹipər].
3. <gai>clobhsa<eng> = a farmyard: [k‘Lousə].
4. <gai>a thogair thu.<eng> One would expect <gai>a thogras tu<eng> ‘which you (will) like’, but Angus MacLellan always uses the past tense in this expression.
5. <gai>’S docha gu bheil.<eng> The vowel of dòcha, ‘more likely’ is regularly shortened in speech, and the word is usually reduced to do’, ’s do’ gu bheil, as occurs frequently in this book.
6. <gai>cuarra, g. cuarradh, dat. cuarraidh,<eng> = quarry, an assimilated loanword. Cp. Seonaidh Caimbeul:
<gai>Théid iad sios far a bheil Ruairi, ’S gheibh iad cuarraidh no metal<eng>
i.e. ‘They’ll go down to Roderick (then the South Uist District clerk) and get work in the quarry or at road-mending’ (Òrain Ghàidhlig le Seonaidh Caimbeul, p. 75).


<gai>7. Catriana Chaluim Chìobair agus Iain Peutan<eng> (Catriana, daughter of Calum the shepherd, and John Beaton)
Recorded on 25/2/60.

1. <gai>leath-amadan,<eng> ‘half-wit’. In the speech of Angus MacLellan and his sister Bean Nìll, the final th (pronounced of course h) of words like leath, math, àmhth, etc., is very clearly heard when a closely associated word follows beginning with a vowel.


<gai>8. Mar a chunnaic mo bhràthair an tòrradh<eng> (How my brother saw a phantom funeral)
Recorded on 1/3/60.

1. <gai>a dh’atharrachadh nan each.<eng> The horses were tethered, and this expression means shifting the tethered horses to fresh ground.
2. <gai>toit,<eng> a small stack of oat or barley sheaves standing in the field, cp. GWSU.
3. <gai>ceanna nan làmh,<eng> ‘the ends of their arms’, i.e. their hands. Làmh means ‘arm’ here (mistranslated in FBM).
4. <gai>dh’fhairlich air a thuigsinn aon fhacal mhàthar dha na thuirt iad,<eng> ‘he was unable to understand a single word of what they said’. Angus MacLellan

[TD 211]

uses màthair ‘mother’ as an intensive in expressions (always negative) like <gai>cha robh stiall mhàthar aodaich air<eng> ‘he hadn’t a single stitch of clothing on him’ (p. 81) and <gai>cha robh teagamh mhàthar agam<eng> ‘I had no doubt whatever’ (p. 176).


<gai>9. Mar a chaidh m’fhoineadh air son coineinich<eng> (How I was fined for shooting a rabbit)
Recorded on 12/1/60, part of Angus MacLellan’s original short autobiography.


<gai>II. AM MILISI<eng> (THE MILITIA)

<gai>10. Mar a liost mi ’sa Mhilisi<eng> (How I joined the Militia)
Recorded on 12/1/60, following No. 8.

1. <gai>clòsaid<eng> = the small room in the middle of a three-roomed Hebridean house. See SSU, p. 207.


11. Pack-drill (Pack-drill in the Militia)
Recorded on 23/2/60, the second part on 6/5/60.

1. When telling this anecdote for the first tune, Angus MacLellan said ‘swallow’. <gai>Annlag-mhara<eng> was used on a later occasion. Apparently this bird is the Stormy Petrel, see Gaelic Words from South Uist.
2. <gai>Tènty<eng> was recorded as the Gaelic for ‘tent’ in Argyllshire by Edward Lhuyd as long ago as 1700. -achan with broad ch is frequent in plurals in South Uist for -aichean.
3.<gai> air a’ chnoc<eng> = outside, out-of-doors. A frequent expression. Mistranslated on p. 149 of SSU.


<gai>12. An turus a thànaig Menagerie a dh’Eilear-nis<eng> (The time the Menagerie came to Inverness)
Recorded on 21/4/60.


<gai>III. TAIGH RÌNIDH<eng> (TIRINIE)

<gai>13. Mar a dh’fhasdadh mi aig tuathanach<eng> (How I engaged with a farmer)
Recorded on 12/1/60, part of Angus MacLellan’s original short autobiography.


<gai>14. An Stoirm Sneachda<eng> (The Snow-storm)
Recorded on 25/2/60.

1. <gai>stàbla.<eng> This form is often used in South Uist, especially by older people, instead of stàbal. Cp. Irish stábla.

14* – 704538 Lochlann. Bd 5

[TD 212]

<gai>15. Obair nan Each<eng> (How I learned to work with horses)

1. <gai>tèarma<eng> = the term, the 28th of May or of November, pay-day on Scottish farms, the dates on which farm workers, who were formerly engaged at hiring fairs, began or terminated their six or twelve months engagements. Cp. Irish téarma.
2. <gai>bosaidh,<eng> Gaelic form of ‘bothy’, the building where unmarried Scottish farm workers used to live, and where they sometimes did their own cooking.
3. <gai>cha ruigea tu.<eng> This form is regular. Cp. Alexander MacDonald, Ais-Eiridh (1751) go’n diona tu, p. 141; na faice tu, p. 14; go d’rinne tu, p. 149.
4. <gai>drilleadh<eng> = covering the turnip seed in the top of earth ridges.
5. <gai>(a’) dol mar pheileir mo bheatha ris,<eng> ‘going all out at it’, cp. p. 45.


<gai>16. Am Fear a rinn am moch-eirigh<eng> (The Early Riser)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

1. <gai>chalcadh iad,<eng> ‘they were caulked’.
2. <gai>muinnleir,<eng> Uist form of <gai>muilleir,<eng> u nasal.


<gai>17. An t-each a b’fheàrr a chunnaig mi<eng> (The best horse I ever saw)
Recorded on 1/3/60.


<gai>18. An làir a thigeadh dhachaigh leatha fhéin<eng> (The mare that came home by herself)

1. Rannoch Moor. This illustrates the tendency of Hebridean speakers to use the English forms of mainland placenames. The correct Gaelic term for Rannoch Moor is <gai>Mòinteach Raineach,<eng> cp. Fr Cyril Dieckhoff’s Dictionary. Similarly Aberfeldy ‘Abair Fealdaidh’ is called <gai>Obair-Pheallaidh<eng> in local Gaelic.


<gai>19. An turus a thug mi gual dha’n àthaidh<eng> (The time I took coal to the kiln)
Recorded on 5/7/60.

1. <gai>coilleadh,<eng> gen. of <gai>coille (dat. coillidh).<eng> Means ‘lignum’ here (and on p. 51) a meaning not given in Dwelly’s Dictionary, but noted by Edward Lhuyd from a Strathspey speaker as long ago as 1704 (see Campbell and Thomson, Edward Lhuyd in the Scottish Highlands, p. 107, 108).


<gai>20. Mar a bha mi falbh leis na beothaichean<eng> (How I took cattle to market)
Recorded on 27/9/60.

1. <gai>ma-sìn.<eng> A horse-drawn vehicle, a trap. Earliest instance recorded by the O.E.D. in this sense is 1687 (Machine).
2. <gai>peileir mo bheatha,<eng> see note no. 5 to Episode 15 above.
3. perhaps <gai>ged ’eireadh<eng> for <gai>ged bheireadh.<eng>

[TD 213]

21. <gai>Na Ceàird a bha barradh nan tuirneap<eng> (How the tinkers topped the turnips)
Recorded on 27/9/60.

1. <gai>barradh<eng> = cutting the tops (leaves) off the turnips, done with a large knife after pulling them out of the ground.


22. <gai>Mr. Raibeart Mingios agus an Tramp<eng> (Mr Robert Menzies and the Tramp)
Recorded on 29/8/61; not included in FBM (probably too late).


23. <gai>A’ Ghàidhlig ann am Peairt<eng> (Gaelic in Perthshire)
Recorded on 6/5/60.

1. <gai>a’ Ghàidhlig ceart<eng> on the tape.
2. See Part I, Note 1.
3. <gai>Dìg<eng> means a ditch in South Uist, a wall in Perthshire.


24. <gai>Na Seann-Chlachan<eng> (Ancient Monuments)
Recorded on 9/8/60.


25. <gai>Cailleach nam Frìth<eng> (Divination)

1. The lady in question, Mrs Angus Cameron, was well known in the 1890s for her ability to psychometrize the position of drowned bodies. She died in 1897. See Folk-Lore, VII 89; and article by Stewart Hunter in Scots Magazine of February 1953, quoting the Rowan Tree Annual of 1907–8 (p. 352). Also Strange Things, pp. 46, 53, 67–8.


26. <gai>Sir Raibeart Mingios agus Sandy<eng> (Sir Robert Menzies and Sandy)
Recorded on 28/4/60.

1. <gai>poitsears.<eng> English plural termination used here, Gaelic plural termination above.
2. <gai>aire-san<eng> is the Uist form of air-san.
3. <gai>geam,<eng> wild game, birds and animals preserved for sport.
4. <gai>lorg mhór choilleadh,<eng> cp. Note 1 to Episode 19.


27. <gai>Mar a chaill Dòmhnall Smios an sporan aige<eng> (How Donald Smith lost his purse)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

1. Miss Mary MacIntosh, then postmistress at Camserney, with whom I discussed this story, said she considered the correct Gaelic for ‘dressmaker’ to be <gai>banalaich.<eng>
2. <gai>tha i comhadach oirnn<eng> = she’s asserting as far as we’re concerned. Cp. Seonaidh Caimbeul, Òrain, p. 55:

[TD 214]

<gai>Comhadaichidh iad air mo mhala
Gun do ghabh mi dhà no trì.<eng>
‘They’ll assert to my face that I took two or three’ (glasses of whisky, when in fact he hadn’t).
3. <gai>cearchaill<eng> = a bolster, a big fat woman.
4. <gai>biod<eng> = a bit [bʎt].
5. <gai>nithinn,<eng> apparently sic, ‘would do’.
6. <gai>Thogadh … shuidheadh.<eng> Angus MacLellan’s fondness for using impersonal forms should be noted. Cp. p. 181 <gai>chualas sgiamh aig a’ bhodach shìos as a’ stàbla; chaidheadh sìos …<eng>
7. <gai>A leabhara!<eng> A common asseveration in Uist and Barra, meaning ‘By the Book’, probably the Missal, see GWSU.
8. <gai>leis bu leis<eng> = whose it was. leis as leis, leis bu leis, the usual way of rendering the genitive of the relative pronoun in Uist and Barra.
9. <gai>mura bi tusa,<eng> sic.


<gai>28. Na Geams a b’fheàrr a chunnaig mi riamh<eng> (The best games I ever saw)
Recorded on 21/4/60.

1. <gai>ga brì = ge bè air bith.<eng> See GWSU <gai>ga brith.<eng>
2. <gai>ag gearradh bhoc,<eng> ‘cutting capers’.
3. <gai>triosdachan,<eng> ‘benches’.
4. Another instance of both Gaelic and English plural terminations being attached to a loanword (cp. the following line in the text). <gai>Fleug<eng> = ‘flake’, which means here a moveable piece of wooden fence, a hurdle.
5. <gai>lobhair e<eng> = ‘he fell asleep’ [Lɔ’ərʹ]. Perhaps from Scots lour, to crouch, huddle. Cp. p. 140.
6. <gai>Clabaigeadh,<eng> clapping, cp. p. 81.


<gai>IV. BOTH RAINICH<eng> (BORENICH)

<gai>29. Mar a chaidh mi do Loch Tumail<eng> (How I went to Loch Tummel)
Recorded on 23/2/61, part also on 9/8/60.

1. <gai>air bàrr nam bròg,<eng> on tiptoe. Presumably Angus MacLellan was first going to say <gai>air a chorra-bhùthag<eng> or <gai>air a chorra-bhìod.<eng>


<gai>30. An oidhche rug a’ bhó<eng> (The night the cow calved)
Recorded on 5/7/60.


<gai>31. Mar a bha amhach ghoirt agam<eng> (The Chill)
Recorded on 23/2/61.

1. <gai>oillsgin,<eng> an oilskin (assimilated loanword).
2. <gai>a h-uile mac oidhche,<eng> every single night. Cp. GWSU, <gai>a h-uile h-aon mhac latha,<eng> every single day.

[TD 215]

3. <gai>na bu dona,<eng> sic.
4. <gai>gus a dhol as mo rian,<eng> ‘about to go off my head’.
5. <gai>lamhan,<eng> pronounced [Lau’ən], so the a is not marked with an accent in this text.
6. <gai>stoirm,<eng> here = noise. Cp. OLMNA p. 41, <gai>Stoirm a’ challtainn ris a’ chuileann;<eng> p. 47, <gai>an cluinn sibh stoirm na cléitheadh;<eng> p. 59, <gai>cluinnt’ an stoirm gam marcrachd<eng> (of horses); p. 25, <gai>stoirm nan gobhar ri taobh na h-abhann.<eng> Cp. O.N. stormr, ‘uproar, tumult’.
7. <gai>criod (cnead),<eng> anything wrong with one’s health, cp. GWSU. One of a number of words used often negative sentences, e.g. <gai>cha n-eil criod air, cha do dh’ich e caiteag, cha do dh’òl e diar, cha d’fhuair e gearradh,<eng> approaching such usages as ne … pas, rien, jamais etc. in French.


<gai>32. An t-Éireannach ’s na postachan<eng> (The Irishman and the posts)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

1. There is a pause after ann. <gai>Am bodach<eng> refers to the Irishman.


<gai>33. Na Basagails<eng> (The Bicycles)
Recorded on 9/8/60. The second part was not included in FBM.

1. <gai>Shàbhail a’ chailleach,<eng> ‘the old lady was saved’. <gai>sàbhail<eng> is both transitive and intransitive in South Uist.
2. <gai>fhuair esan a chriùdadh gu h-olc,<eng> ‘he was badly hurt’.


<gai>34. Bann-Uachdaran Fhionn-Chaisteil<eng> (The Lady of Fincastle)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

In the translation in FBM, ‘Mrs Barber’ should read ‘Mrs Barbour’. The fête described by Angus MacLellan here was reported in the local press at the time.

1. <gai>clapaigeadh,<eng> see note no. 6, Section 28.


<gai>35. An Tramp a thànaig a Loch Tumail<eng> (The tramp who came to Loch Tummel)
Recorded on 5/7/60.

1. <gai>stiall mhàthar aodaich,<eng> cp. note no. 4 to Section 8.
2. <gai>aire-san,<eng> see note no. 2 to Section 26.
3. <gai>beula fodair<eng> = ‘a bale of straw’.
4. <gai>crùgan,<eng> ‘a handful’, cp. Irish crúca.


<gai>V. ROWARDENNAN

36. Mar a chaidh mi a Rowardennan<eng> (How I went to Rowardennan)
The first three paragraphs are part of the original recording of 25/2/60, that was translated in SSU. The rest of this section was recorded on 12/1/60 on another tape.

[TD 216]

1. <gai>bha an citsinn gus a dhol fo theine air fad aca,<eng> ‘they were going to turn the kitchen completely upside down’.
2. <gai>gu math brothach,<eng> ‘pretty crossly’. Cp. GWSU.
3. <gai>thànaig rachd ’na amhaich,<eng> ‘a lump came into his throat’.


<gai>37. Rob Baird agus Bean Uilleim<eng> (Rob Baird and William’s wife)
Recorded on 23/2/61.

1. <gai>nam mòls,<eng> ‘of the moles’. Assimilated loanword with English plural termination. There are no moles in the Outer Hebrides, so the Gaelic terms for them, e.g. <gai>famh<eng> or <gai>toranach<eng> (recorded by Lhuyd in Argyll and from an Inverness-shire speaker in 1699 and 1704 respectively) or <gai>famhan<eng> (Dieckhoff, Dictionary based on the Glengarry dialect) are little known there, if at all.
2. <gai>mu choinneamh uinneig,<eng> i.e., (working) opposite a window, where someone can keep an eye on you.
3. <gai>air a’ chnoc<eng> very clearly means ‘outside’ here.


<gai>38. Loch Lomonn<eng> (Loch Lomond)
Recorded on 1/3/60 and 21/4/60, the first part in response to my question about what fishes there were in the loch.

1. <gai>taobh an iar.<eng> The west side of South Uist is referred to.
2. <gai>stuagh marbh.<eng> A swell breaking on the shore when there is no wind.
3. <gai>o bhlad gu blad,<eng> literally ‘from an open mouth to an open mouth’, i.e., of a small boat, rocking from the trough of one wave to the trough of the next. I am obliged to Mr John MacInnes M.B.E., Daliburgh, for the explanation of this expression, which is not to be found in the dictionaries.


<gai>39. Na Poitsears<eng> (The Poachers)
Recorded on 27/2/60.

1. <gai>deargadh éisg,<eng> cp. GWSU.
2. <gai>Bha an seo an cadal ’gam thoir’ fhìn a mach,<eng> ‘at this point sleep was overcoming me myself’.
3. <gai>plantain<eng> = a plantation, assimilated loanword.


<gai>40. Sandy Blair agus an t-Iasad<eng> (Sandy Blair and the Loan)
Recorded on 9/8/60. This section reminds one of the proverb <gai>‘na biodh an t-iasad a’ rànaich a’ tighinn dhachaigh’<eng> ‘let not the loan come home weeping’ recorded by Fr Allan McDonald in South Uist.

1. <gai>a theans<eng> = by chance.
2. <gai>seamad<eng> = semmit, a flannel undershirt.
3. <gai>bha mise ’ga thoir’ as mo chorp fhéin,<eng> I was taking it out of myself, was exhausting myself doing his job for him.

[TD 217]

4. <gai>a bha ’gam ruighinn,<eng> ‘that was getting me down’. See GWSU under ràinig.
5. <gai>an waits.<eng> The watch for the poachers described in section 40, which Blair had instigated and Angus MacLellan had sabotaged, fearing that if the poachers were caught his own friends would get into trouble.
6. <gai>leum an nàdar orm,<eng> ‘I lost my temper’.
7. <gai>’s e leum chon a’ chnoic,<eng> ‘and he dashing off’.
8. <gai>tarplain,<eng> a tarpaulin (assimilated loanword).
9. <gai>bha e dona leam.<eng> In Uist tha can be used with le, contrary to literary usage. Perhaps this originated with <gai>tha dùil a’m<eng> (for agam) ‘I expect’ being taken as tha dùil leam.
10. <gai>tha mi air son mo lamhan a sheasamh dhuibh,<eng> ‘I want to stand you a drink’.


<gai>41. An turus a thànaig an gual a Rowardennan<eng> (The time coal came to Rowardennan)
Recorded on 5/7/60.

1. <gai>or ’o<eng> (also written orra in some texts), colloquial for <gai>air do,<eng> ‘on thy’.
2. <gai>ach bu dig an latha<eng> ‘but the day would never come’. Cp. <gai>nar bu tig an dìle<eng> ‘may the day never come’, GWSU p. 99.
3. ball here is the English word.
4. <gai>dùcadh<eng> = wading.
5. <gai>gun diùc air sianar,<eng> ‘without six men being insufficient’, i.e. six men were not enough for the job.


<gai>42. An turus a thug mi dhachaigh an togsaid pharafain<eng> (The time I took home the barrel of paraffin)
Recorded on 5/7/60.


<gai>43. Mar a mharbh mi an coileach dubh<eng> (How I shot the blackcock)
Recorded on 5/7/60

1. <gai>tàr(r)<eng> means (a) to go away (b) to manage (to do something). Cp. p. 138, and GWSU.


<gai>44. Obair na deoch ann an Rowardennan<eng> (The drink trade at Rowardennan)
Recorded on 21/4/60.

1. The tape sounds like <gai>an ceann dha treiseig, or an ceann dhaibh treiseig.<eng>
2. ‘Spirit’ was the term for the better kind of whisky, costing 4/6 a bottle.

[TD 218]

<gai>45. An turus a ghlanadh seileir Rowardennan<eng> (The time the cellar was cleared out)
Recorded on 21/4/60.

1. Idiomatic use of is. ‘Clear of the hotel’. Cp. <gai>ullamh is e<eng> ‘finished with him’, p. 170, and GWSU.
2. a fhrock, his frock coat.


<gai>46. Mar a rinn mi an t-Éireannach mór a chur a mach as a bhàr<eng> (How I got the big Irishman to leave the bar)
Recorded on 5/7/60.

1. morning = a glass of liquor taken before breakfast (Scots: Chambers’s Scots Dictionary).
2. <gai>na deoch.<eng> Genitive same as nominative here.


<gai>47. Am fear a chaidh ceàrr as a’ cheann<eng> (The man who went wrong in the head)
Recorded on 27/2/60.

1. <gai>troch,<eng> a trough (Scots).
2. <gai>bha e straointe fosgailte,<eng> it (the door) was ajar.


<gai>48. No. 13 ann an Rowardennan<eng> (Room number 13 at Rowardennan)
Recorded on 1/3/60, and again on 24/4/60.

1. <gai>bha an taigh a’ soundadh dha,<eng> the house was resounding to it. ‘Sound’, noun and verb, have also been borrowed by Welsh (sŵn, swnio), and ‘soundadh’ might well be treated as an assimilated loanword in Scottish Gaelic.


<gai>49. An turus a dh’fhalbh mi a sheòladh còmhla ri Uilleam ’s a bhean<eng> (The time I took William and his wife for a sail)
Recorded on 21/4/60.

1. <gai>nach ann ort tha ’n oak,<eng> i.e. aren’t you a hard fellow.
2. <gai>thill siod,<eng> ‘yon (man) came back’.
3. <gai>ach tarraing air an taigh,<eng> i.e. William and his wife went straight to their own house at Ross, without going into the Rowardennan Hotel at all.


<gai>50. Uilleam agus na Bòcain<eng> (William and the Apparitions)
Recorded on 25/2/60.

1. <gai>cha tàradh esan e fhéin a shìneadh as a’ leabaidh,<eng> ‘he hadn’t managed to stretch himself on the bed’.
2. The word for ‘horn’ is often pronounced as if spelt <gai>adhraic,<eng> in South Uist and Barra, but Angus MacLellan said <gai>adhairc.<eng>
3. <gai>a staigh fo fhear dha na bùird.<eng> These words were spoken rapidly, and fo

[TD 219]

fhear is not very clear on the tape, but it is evident that William was underneath the table, not on it, as was mistakenly said in FBM.
4. <gai>na faigheadh e<eng> seems to be clear on the tape, though one would expect <gai>cha n-fhaigheadh,<eng> or <gai>cà ’m faigheadh,<eng> the latter suggested by Mrs MacPhee.
5. A striking clock interferes with the audibility of the tape here.


<gai>51. Uilleam agus a’ Phàirc thuirneap<eng> (William and the field of turnips)
Recorded on 27/2/60.

1. <gai>gu dé bu sgial dha’n àite bhith mar seo,<eng> ‘what was the meaning of the place being like this?’ (Sgial used for ciall in this expression).
2. Another instance of the intransitive use of <gai>sàbhail.<eng>
3. These lines were spoken very rapidly, indeed excitedly, being the climax of the story. What had happened was this: the fence which Angus MacLellan had made to protect the turnip clamps in the field, was made of wood; he had driven wooden fence posts 5½ feet long into the ground, and had nailed to them horizontally a number of ‘slabs’, rough pieces of timber produced at the sawmill from the outside of trees, and used for fencing. William had taken a hammer and knocked out the slabs between two of the fence posts from the inside, thus leaving an opening <gai>(cachaileith)<eng> through which the cattle which he was supposed to be looking after could come and help themselves to the turnips, thus saving him from the trouble of taking food to them.
4. <gai>stuagh liath air—a mach o’n dorus.<eng> These are the words which appear to be on the tape, referring to a cloud of mouldy dust that came out when Angus MacLellan opened the door of the cowshed. At the time that I was translating FBM, someone suggested that the words were <gai>bha struth do bhuachar a’ tighinn a mach o’n dorus<eng> ‘a stream of cow-dung was coming out of the door’, but that cannot be right, as Angus goes on to say that he didn’t know when a cattle beast had last been inside.
5. <gai>le wire bhioraich,<eng> ‘with barbed wire’. Wire should really be considered an assimilated loanword in Sc. Gaelic by now. Like <gai>feans<eng> it is feminine.
6. <gai>gabhaidh cuarra an rathaid mhóir,<eng> i.e. let them go and camp in a quarry by the high road like tinkers.
7. Note the distinction, which is always made in Angus MacLellan’s Gaelic, between <gai>seirbhiseach<eng> ‘a farm worker, farm servant’, and <gai>searbhana<eng> ‘a domestic servant’.


<gai>52. Uilleam agus am Fiadh<eng> (William and the Deer)
Recorded on 27/2/60, no pause between this anecdote and the preceding one.

1. <gai>agus croit oirre<eng> ‘and she bulging’.
2. <gai>biodh e fhéin a’ deanamh lagh ribh,<eng> we might say ‘and he can read you the riot act’. Cp. lagh in GWSU.

[TD 220]

<gai>53. Mar a dh’fhàg mi am bodach Kane<eng> (How I left old Kane)
Recorded on 23/2/60.

1. <gai>(a) shìomaineachadh nan dais,<eng> to put heather ropes on the haystacks.
2. <gai>cha robh mise an taing mo sgrìonadh,<eng> ‘I was not obliged [to you] for screening me’.


<gai>VI. DAIL-MHÀILIDH<eng> (DALMALLY)

<gai>54. Mar a chaidh mi a Dhail-Mhàilidh<eng> (How I went to Dalmally)
Recorded on 27/4/61.

1. Angus MacLellan said <gai>Canaidh,<eng> ‘Canna’, on the tape, but local inquiry made it certain that Calum MacNicol was from the neighbouring island of Eigg.


<gai>55. An treobhaiche a b’fheàrr<eng> (The ploughing-match)
Recorded on 25/4/61.

1. A well-known proverb.
2. <gai>fiaraing,<eng> Scots feering, ‘the furrow drawn out to mark the ‘riggs’ before ploughing the whole field’ (Chambers’s Scots Dictionary).


<gai>56. An Làir<eng> (The Mare)
Recorded on 1/3/60.


<gai>57. An Driver a b’fheàrr ann an Alba<eng> (The best driver in Scotland)
Recorded on 25/4/61.

1. MacColl in English.


<gai>58. A’ chiad mhotor a chunna mi<eng> (The first motor-car I ever saw)
Recorded on 25/4/61, in the middle of a piece on sea fishing, in response to a question of mine which was really intended to ask about motor boats.


<gai>59. Dòmhnall Beag Mac Niocail<eng> (Little Donald MacNicol)
Recorded on 1/2/64, after the publication of FBM.

1. <gai>seugars,<eng> the shakers of the threshing mill, inside which mill Donald MacNicol had hidden.


<gai>60. Dòmhnall Beag agus am Buntàta<eng> (Little Donald and the potatoes)
Recorded on 1/2/64, the last autobiographical anecdote I recorded from Angus MacLellan.

1. <gai>drìleag,<eng> cp. GWSU <gai>grìolagan,<eng> small potatoes.

[TD 221]

<gai>61. Am meàrlach<eng> (The thief)
Recorded on 9/8/60.


62. Anderson agus John Moffatt (Anderson and John Moffatt)
Recorded on 9/8/60.


<gai>63. Anderson agus am pige<eng> (Anderson and the jar of whisky)
Recorded on 9/8/60.


64. An Tramp (The Tramp)
Recorded on 25/2/60.

1. Donald MacLeod, Dòmhnall Nìll, was living and working as gardener on the Isle of Canna when the writer went there in 1938. He had gone to Dalmally a year after Angus MacLellan did, around 1900.
2. <gai>ma tha beag leat na fhuair thu,<eng> if you don’t feel it much what you got. Another instance of the use of tha + le.
3. <gai>ghabhadh esan a<eng> chare, ‘he’d take care of him’.
4. <gai>Cha bu cheamha leam air mo thuarasdal e,<eng> ‘I wouldn’t for all my wages’. See Ceamh in GWSU.
5. <gai>’s ann a mhuinntir a’ Chinn-a-Tuath a bha e,<eng> he belonged to North Uist.


<gai>65. Mar a dh’fhalbh mi a Dail-Mhàilidh<eng> (How I left Dalmally)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

1. <gai>bha i ’n sin aige as a’ ghrùnnd,<eng> he owned it outright, he was a freeholder.


<gai>VII. UIBHIST A RITHIST<eng> (UIST AGAIN)

<gai>66. An t-Iasgach<eng> (Fishing)
First two paragraphs were part of recording made on 12/1/60; the next three (story of how the boat went dry on the rock while Angus MacLellan and Johnny MacAskill were asleep) on 23/2/60; the next three (story of the night Angus got soaked) on 6/5/60; the rest of this section on 25/4/61, mostly in response to my questioning about fishing in the old days.

1. ‘gale’ is actually assimilated to Gaelic phonetics, pronounced [gʹælʹ].
2. <gai>flanainn,<eng> a flannel undershirt; <gai>flanainnean,<eng> a set of flannel underwear.
3. <gai>na Tròidich,<eng> the East coast fishermen. Cp. GWSU, <gai>Tròdhsach,<eng> possibly ‘a Montrose man’.
4. The merchants only paid the price of half a lobster for a lobster with only one claw, however big the lobster was. Cp. Òran nan Giomach, by Seonaidh Caimbeul:

15 – 704538 Lochlann. Bd 5

[TD 222]

<gai>Agus an déidh a h-uile rud
Nuair a théid an cruinneachadh,
Ged a dhianainn dusan dheth
Bidh cus dhiu air leath-spòige.<eng>
‘And, after everything, when they (i.e. the lobsters) have been collected, though I would make up a dozen, many of them would have only one claw.’ (Òrain, p. 11)


<gai>67. Sealgaireachd<eng> (Hunting)
Recorded on 28/4/60.

1. <gai>Cù mór coilearach dubh,<eng> a big black dog marked white round the neck.


<gai>68. An Dobharchu<eng> (The King Otter)
Recorded on 27/2/60.

1. <gai>ligidh,<eng> a canal or open drain.
2. <gai>bòrd an locha,<eng> the verge of the loch.
3. For the qualities attributed to the skin of the King Otter, see note on p. 64 of Campbell and Thomson, Edward Lhuyd in the Scottish Highlands; also Carmina Gadelica, IV 2.


<gai>69. Alasdair Mór ’s an stoirm<eng> (Alasdair Mór and the storm)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

Alasdair MacIntyre, ‘Alasdair Mór mac Iain Dheirg’, who lived at the back of Ben More in South Uist, was a famous Gaelic storyteller. Thirteen of the forty-two stories translated in SSU were learnt by Angus MacLellan from him. His son, the late Donald MacIntyre, maintained the tradition, and recorded a number of stories for the Rev Fr John MacLean.


<gai>70. Alasdair Mór ’s na h-othaisgean<eng> (Alasdair Mór and the hoggs)
Recorded on 9/8/60.

1. <gai>smod do chil,<eng> ‘a spot of keel’. Keel is colouring matter, red, blue, or green, used to mark sheep in different ways so that different classes can be distinguished. A hogg is a sheep between the age of 6 and 18 months.
2. <gai>urball,<eng> i.e. <gai>urball na seacaid,<eng> the tail of his jacket, which he had taken care to dip in the river, to make it appear that he had been out in the rain.


<gai>71. Mar a ghléidh mi mo chuid chaorach<eng> (How I managed to keep my sheep)
Recorded on 23/2/60.

1. <gai>Crislein<eng> = Christie.
2. <gai>Hùidseadh ort,<eng> ‘get a move on’.
3. <gai>a h-uile mèath dhiu,<eng> ‘every bleat of them’.
4. <gai>an dab aca,<eng> ‘the whole lot of them’.

[TD 223]

<gai>72. Cùirt nan Con<eng> (Our dogs in the dock)
Recorded on 23/2/61.

1. <gai>Bha iad a’ faighinn lìbhrigeadh unnt’,<eng> i.e., when they obtained entry into the crofts, when they got their leases.
2. <gai>bha mise air fear dhiu,<eng> ‘I was one of them’.
3. <gai>nach eil sgrìobht’ ann am fearann idir,<eng> ‘who are not registered landholders at all’.
4. Andrew MacElfrish. He was also clerk to the South Uist School Board, see F. G. Rea, A School in South Uist, pp. 132, 159.
5. The last paragraph here comes at the end of the recording made on 12/1/60, and the last sentence of it is a remark made by Angus MacLellan after the recording was finished.

[TD 224]

BIBLIOGRAPHY

In view of the importance of the dialect of South Uist for students of Scottish Gaelic, the following list of texts, dialect studies, and lexicographical work connected with South Uist Gaelic may be of interest.

BORGSTRØM, Professor CARL HJ., The Dialects of the Outer Hebrides (Norsk Tidsskrift for Sprogvidenskap Suppl. Vol. 1, Oslo 1940). Includes material noted at Lochboisdale.
CAMPBELL, J. F., Leabhar na Féinne. London, 1872. Heroic ballads; some originally taken in South Uist.
—— West Highland Tales. Four volumes. Paisley, 1890 (second edition).
—— More West Highland Tales. Edited by J. G. MacKay. Two volumes, Edinburgh, 1940 and 1960. These six volumes of stories collected by J. F. Campbell and his collaborators (there are more in MS) contain many items taken down in South Uist.
CAMPBELL, J. L. and COLLINSON, FRANCIS, Hebridean Folksongs; the MacCormick Collection of Waulking Songs. Oxford, 1969; waulking song texts written down by Donald MacCormick, Kilphedir, South Uist, in 1893 (tunes added from recordings [Volume II in preparation]).
CAMPBELL, J. L. and MACINNES, JOHN, Òrain Ghàidlig le Seonaidh Caimbeul (Dunfermline, 1936) (Gaelic poems by John Campbell, the South Lochboisdale bard, 1859–1944).
CAMPBELL, J. L., Sia Sgialachdan, Six Gaelic Stories from South Uist and Barra (Edinburgh, 1938).
CAMPBELL, Mrs. NEIL (sister of Angus MacLellan), A’ Chiad uair a dh’ fhàg mi Uibhist (‘The first time I left Uist’) Gairm No. 32 pp. 314–317. Transcribed from a tape recording made by J. L. Campbell.
—— An Dàrna àite dha’n deach mi (‘The second place I went to’) Gairm No. 33 p. 51. Transcribed from a recording by J. L. Campbell. Mrs. Neil Campbell was 100 years old on August 4th, 1967.
CARMICHAEL, ALEXANDER, Carmina Gadelica (five volumes; Edinburgh 1928, 1940, 1941, 1954). Contains much material collected in South Uist.
CRAIG, K. C., Orain Luaidh Màiri Nighean Alasdair (Edinburgh 1949; traditional waulking song texts) (OLMNA).
—— Sgialachdan Dhunnchaidh (Edinburgh, 1944; five heroic stories taken down from the late Duncan MacDonald, Peninerine 1879–1950).
HENDERSON, Rev. GEORGE, D. D., Ciad Mhac Riogh Africa (The first son of the King of Africa) (Guth na Bliadhna II 353: taken down from John MacKinnon, Daliburgh. One of a considerable number of stories and ballads Henderson took down in South Uist in 1892, most of which remain unpublished).

[TD 225]

—— Leabhar nan Gleann (Inverness, 1898) (Contains inter alia the texts of songs collected in South Uist in 1892).
MCDONALD, Rev. Fr. ALLAN, Bàrdachd Mhgr. Ailein (Edinburgh, 1965).
—— Gaelic words & Expressions from South Uist and Eriskay (Dublin, 1958) (GWSU).
—— Leabhar-Latha (Published in Gairm, vols 1 & 2) (Diary kept in Gaelic in March 1898).
MAC ’ILL ’FHINNEIN, GORDON, Gàidhlig Uibhist a Deas (Dublin Institute for Advanced Studies, 1966).
MACINTYRE, DONALD (Dòmhnall Ruadh), Sporan Dhòmhnaill (original poems, edited by the Rev. Somhairle MacMillan and published by the Scottish Gaelic Texts Society; Edinburgh, 1968).
MACLEAN, Dr. CALUM, An Donn Ghuailleann (version of the ‘Cattle Raid of Cooley’ recorded from Angus MacLellan in 1959. See Gairm No. 29 p. 67, and Arv vol. XV pp. 160–180, 1959).
MACLELLAN, ANGUS, Stories from South Uist, translated by J. L. Campbell (London, 1961, SSU) (particular Gaelic words and expressions noted).
MACRAE, PEIGI (b.c. 1869) Catriana nighean Eachainn (‘Catriana, daughter of Hector’). Catriana was a seer. Transcribed from a recording made by J. L. Campbell. See Gairm No. 9, p. 58, and Scots Magazine of October 1955.
SHAW, MARGARET FAY, Folksongs and Folklore of South Uist (London, 1955).

[TD 226]

GLOSSARIAL INDEX

This Index includes words not found in Edward Dwelly’s Illustrated Gaelic Dictionary, words used in senses not recorded by Dwelly, and dialect forms. It provides some further contexts for words found in Fr Allan McDonald’s Gaelic Words from South Uist.

List of Abbreviations
adj.		adjective
adv.		adverb
dat.		dative
dial.		dialect
f.		feminine
gen.		genitive
intr.		intransitive
m.		masculine
n.		noun
neg.		negative
per.		person
pl.		plural
pn.		pronoun
dem. pn.	demonstrative pronoun
tr.		transative
vb.		verb
vbl. n.		verbal noun

ELSH		J. L. Campbell and Derick Thomson, Edward Lhuyd in the Scottish Highlands, Oxford, 1963.
S.C.		Seonaidh Caimbeul, Orain Ghàidhlig, Dunfermline, 1936.

Other abbreviations can be found in the Bibliography. Numbers not preceded by the word note refer to pages.
